</ul>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.1em;font-style:italic;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Disclogo1.svg"><img alt="Disclogo1.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="254" data-file-width="169" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Disclogo1.svg/10px-Disclogo1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Disclogo1.svg/16px-Disclogo1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Disclogo1.svg/21px-Disclogo1.svg.png 2x" width="10"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Discrimination" title="Portal:Discrimination">Discrimination portal</a></td>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template talk:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Discrimination_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Sex-selective abortion</b> is the practice of terminating a pregnancy based upon the predicted sex of the infant. The selective <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> of female fetuses is most common in areas where cultural norms value male children over female children, especially in parts of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> (particularly in countries such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>), as well as in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodkind_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodkind-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gettis_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gettis-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-czgparis_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-czgparis-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sex-selective abortion affects the <a href="/wiki/Human_sex_ratio" title="Human sex ratio">human sex ratio</a>—the relative number of males to females in a given age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coevolution_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-coevolution-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Studies and reports focusing on sex-selective abortion are predominantly statistical; they assume that birth sex ratio—the overall ratio of boys and girls at birth for a regional population, is an indicator of sex-selective abortion. This assumption has been questioned by some scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James-JournalofEndocrinology_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-James-JournalofEndocrinology-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scholars who support the assumption, suggest that the expected birth sex ratio range is 103 to 107 males to females at birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpa2012_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa2012-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kseb_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-kseb-8">[8]</a></sup> Countries considered to have significant practices of sex-selective abortion are those with birth sex ratios of 108 and above (<a href="/wiki/Female_infanticide" title="Female infanticide">selective abortion of females</a>), and 102 and below (<b>selective abortion of males</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpa2012_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa2012-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_sex_ratio_at_birth">Human sex ratio at birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Human sex ratio at birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_sex_ratio" title="Human sex ratio">Human sex ratio</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abortion_Laws.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Abortion_Laws.svg/400px-Abortion_Laws.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Abortion_Laws.svg/600px-Abortion_Laws.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Abortion_Laws.svg/800px-Abortion_Laws.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abortion_Laws.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Human_sex_ratio" title="Human sex ratio">human sex ratio</a> at birth can vary for natural reasons as well as from sex-selective abortion. In many nations abortion is legal (see above map, dark blue).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sex-selective abortion affects the <a href="/wiki/Human_sex_ratio" title="Human sex ratio">human sex ratio</a>—the relative number of males to females in a given age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coevolution_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-coevolution-4">[4]</a></sup> Studies and reports that discuss sex-selective abortion are based on the assumption that birth sex ratio—the overall ratio of boys and girls at birth for a regional population, is an indicator of sex-selective abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James-JournalofEndocrinology_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-James-JournalofEndocrinology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The natural human sex ratio at birth was estimated, in a 2002 study, to be close to 106 boys to 100 girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Human sex ratio at birth that is significantly different from 106 is often assumed to be correlated to the prevalence and scale of sex-selective abortion. This assumption is controversial, and a subject of continuing scientific studies.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_or_low_human_sex_ratio_implies_sex-selective_abortion">High or low human sex ratio implies sex-selective abortion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: High or low human sex ratio implies sex-selective abortion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One school of scholars suggest that any birth sex ratio of boys to girls that is outside of the normal 105-107 range, necessarily implies sex-selective abortion. These scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> claim that both the sex ratio at birth and the population sex ratio are remarkably constant in human populations. Significant deviations in birth sex ratios from the normal range can only be explained by manipulation, that is sex-selective abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KlausenWink_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-KlausenWink-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a widely cited article,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen1990_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen1990-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a> compared the birth sex ratio in Europe (106) and United States (105) with those in Asia (107+) and argued that the high sex ratios in East Asia, West Asia and South Asia may be due to excessive female mortality. Sen pointed to research that had shown that if men and women receive similar nutritional and medical attention and good health care then females have better survival rates, and it is the male which is the genetically fragile sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kseb_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-kseb-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sen estimated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missing_women_of_Asia" title="Missing women of Asia">'missing women' from extra women who would have survived in Asia</a> if it had the same ratio of women to men as Europe and United States. According to Sen, the high birth sex ratio over decades, implies a female shortfall of 11% in Asia, or over 100 million women as missing from the 3 billion combined population of South Asia, West Asia, North Africa and China.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_or_low_human_sex_ratio_may_be_natural">High or low human sex ratio may be natural</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: High or low human sex ratio may be natural">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other scholars question whether birth sex ratio outside 103-107 can be due to natural reasons. William James and others<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James-JournalofEndocrinology_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-James-JournalofEndocrinology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> suggest that conventional assumptions have been:</p>
<ul>
<li>there are equal numbers of X and Y chromosomes in mammalian sperms</li>
<li>X and Y stand equal chance of achieving conception</li>
<li>therefore equal number of male and female zygotes are formed, and that</li>
<li>therefore any variation of sex ratio at birth is due to sex selection between conception and birth.</li>
</ul>
<p>James cautions that available scientific evidence stands against the above assumptions and conclusions. He reports that there is an excess of males at birth in almost all human populations, and the natural sex ratio at birth is usually between 102 and 108. However the ratio may deviate significantly from this range for natural reasons such as early marriage and fertility, teenage mothers, average maternal age at birth, paternal age, age gap between father and mother, late births, ethnicity, social and economic stress, warfare, environmental and hormonal effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James-JournalofEndocrinology_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-James-JournalofEndocrinology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> This school of scholars support their alternate hypothesis with historical data when modern sex-selection technologies were unavailable, as well as birth sex ratio in sub-regions, and various ethnic groups of developed economies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rjhmam_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-rjhmam-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tvlkjt_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-tvlkjt-17">[17]</a></sup> They suggest that direct abortion data should be collected and studied, instead of drawing conclusions indirectly from human sex ratio at birth.</p>
<p>James' hypothesis is supported by historical birth sex ratio data before technologies for ultrasonographic sex-screening were discovered and commercialized in the 1960s and 1970s, as well by reverse abnormal sex ratios currently observed in Africa. Michel Garenne reports that many African nations have, over decades, witnessed birth sex ratios below 100, that is more girls are born than boys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> have reported birth sex ratios between 94 and 99, which is quite different than the presumed 104 to 106 as natural human birth sex ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Graunt" title="John Graunt">John Graunt</a> noted that in London over a 35-year period in the 17th century (1628–62),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> the birth sex ratio was 1.07; while Korea's historical records suggest a birth sex ratio of 1.13, based on 5 million births, in 1920s over a 10-year period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Other historical records from Asia too support James hypothesis. For example, Jiang et al. claim that the birth sex ratio in China was 116–121 over a 100-year period in the late 18th and early 19th centuries; in the 120–123 range in the early 20th century; falling to 112 in the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_on_human_sex_ratio_at_birth">Data on human sex ratio at birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Data on human sex ratio at birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_sex_ratio" title="List of countries by sex ratio">List of countries by sex ratio</a></div>
<p>In the United States, the sex ratios at birth over the period 1970–2002 were 105 for the white non-Hispanic population, 104 for Mexican Americans, 103 for African Americans and Native Americans, and 107 for mothers of Chinese or Filipino ethnicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Among Western European countries c. 2001, the ratios ranged from 104 to 107.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In the aggregated results of 56 Demographic and Health Surveys<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> in African countries, the birth sex ratio was found to be 103, though there is also considerable country-to-country, and year-to-year variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2005 study, U.S. Department of Health and Human Services reported sex ratio at birth in the United States from 1940 over 62 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCHS_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCHS-30">[30]</a></sup> This statistical evidence suggested the following: For mothers having their first baby, the total sex ratio at birth was 106 overall, with some years at 107. For mothers having babies after the first, this ratio consistently decreased with each additional baby from 106 towards 103. The age of the mother affected the ratio: the overall ratio was 105 for mothers aged 25 to 35 at the time of birth; while mothers who were below the age of 15 or above 40 had babies with a sex ratio ranging between 94 and 111, and a total sex ratio of 104. This United States study also noted that American mothers of Hawaiian, Filipino, Chinese, Cuban and Japanese ethnicity had the highest sex ratio, with years as high as 114 and average sex ratio of 107 over the 62-year study period. Outside of United States, European nations with extensive birth records, such as Finland, report similar variations in birth sex ratios over a 250-year period, that is from 1751 to 1997 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tvlkjt_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-tvlkjt-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, according to CIA estimates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-31">[31]</a></sup> the countries with the highest birth sex ratio were <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a> (126), <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (115), <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> (113), <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (112), <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> (112), <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> (111), <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> (111), <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> (111), <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> (110), <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a> (110), <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">San Marino</a> (110), <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> (108), <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> (108), <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a> (108) and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Macedonia</a> (108). The lowest boys to girls birth sex ratios in 2016 were estimated by CIA for<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nauru" title="Nauru">Nauru</a> (83), <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> (94), <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> (101), <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> (101), <a href="/wiki/Aruba" title="Aruba">Aruba</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> (102), <a href="/wiki/Saint_Kitts_and_Nevis" title="Saint Kitts and Nevis">Saint Kitts and Nevis</a> (102), and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> (102).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_reliability">Data reliability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Data reliability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The estimates for birth sex ratios, and thus derived sex-selective abortion, are a subject of dispute as well. For example, United States' CIA projects<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-cia-32">[32]</a></sup> the birth sex ratio for <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> to be 106, while the Switzerland's Federal Statistical Office that tracks actual live births of boys and girls every year, reports the latest birth sex ratio for Switzerland as 107.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Other variations are more significant; for example, CIA projects<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-cia-32">[32]</a></sup> the birth sex ratio for <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> to be 105, United Nations FPA office claims<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> the birth sex ratio for Pakistan to be 110, while the government of Pakistan claims its average birth sex ratio is 111.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two most studied nations with high sex ratio and sex-selective abortion are China and India. The CIA estimates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-cia-32">[32]</a></sup> a birth sex ratio of 112 for both in recent years. However, The World Bank claims the birth sex ratio for China in 2009 was 120 boys for every 100 girls;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> while United Nations FPA estimates China's 2011 birth sex ratio to be 118.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpaaugust2012_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfpaaugust2012-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>For India, the United Nations FPA claims a birth sex ratio of 111 over 2008–10 period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpaaugust2012_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-unfpaaugust2012-38">[38]</a></sup> while The World Bank and India's official 2011 Census reports a birth sex ratio of 108.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teconomist_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-teconomist-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> These variations and data reliability is important as a rise from 108 to 109 for India, or 117 to 118 for China, each with large populations, represent a possible sex-selective abortion of about 100,000 girls.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prenatal_sex_discernment">Prenatal sex discernment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Prenatal sex discernment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_sex_discernment" title="Prenatal sex discernment">Prenatal sex discernment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boy.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="234" data-file-width="276" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Boy.JPG/220px-Boy.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Boy.JPG 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boy.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ultrasonography image showing the fetus is a boy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The earliest post-implantation test, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_free_fetal_DNA" title="Cell free fetal DNA">cell free fetal DNA</a> testing, involves taking a blood sample from the mother and isolating the small amount of fetal <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> that can be found within it. When performed after week seven of pregnancy, this method is about 98% accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Obstetric_ultrasonography" title="Obstetric ultrasonography">Obstetric ultrasonography</a>, either transvaginally or transabdominally, checks for various markers of fetal sex. It can be performed at or after week 12 of pregnancy. At this point, <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> of fetal sexes can be correctly determined, according to a 2001 study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazza_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-mazza-43">[43]</a></sup> Accuracy for males is approximately 50% and for females almost 100%. When performed after week 13 of pregnancy, ultrasonography gives an accurate result in almost 100% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazza_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-mazza-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most invasive measures are <a href="/wiki/Chorionic_villus_sampling" title="Chorionic villus sampling">chorionic villus sampling</a> (CVS) and <a href="/wiki/Amniocentesis" title="Amniocentesis">amniocentesis</a>, which involve testing of the chorionic villus (found in the placenta) and <a href="/wiki/Amniotic_fluid" title="Amniotic fluid">amniotic fluid</a>, respectively. Both techniques typically test for <a href="/wiki/Chromosome_abnormality" title="Chromosome abnormality">chromosomal disorders</a> but can also reveal the sex of the child and are performed early in the pregnancy. However, they are often more expensive and more dangerous than blood sampling or ultrasonography, so they are seen less frequently than other sex determination techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Availability</dt>
</dl>
<p>China launched its first ultrasonography machine in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup> Chinese health care clinics began introducing ultrasound technologies that could be used to determine prenatal sex in 1982. By 1991, Chinese companies were producing 5,000 ultrasonography machines per year. Almost every rural and urban hospital and family planning clinics in China had a good quality sex discernment equipment by 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The launch of ultrasonography technology in India too occurred in 1979, but its expansion was slower than China. Ultrasound sex discernment technologies were first introduced in major cities of India in the 1980s, its use expanded in India's urban regions in the 1990s, and became widespread in the 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maydrgermany_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-maydrgermany-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence_of_sex-selective_abortion">Prevalence of sex-selective abortion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Prevalence of sex-selective abortion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">

</table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Missing_women_of_China" title="Missing women of China">Missing women of China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Female_infanticide_in_China" title="Female infanticide in China">Female infanticide in China</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_administrative_divisions_by_gender_ratio" title="List of Chinese administrative divisions by gender ratio">List of Chinese administrative divisions by gender ratio</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg/250px-G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg/375px-G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg/500px-G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:G106-Dajipu-to-Daye-hills-0068.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A roadside slogan calls motorists to crack down on medically unnecessary antenatal sex identification and sex-selective pregnancy termination practices. (<a href="/wiki/Daye,_Hubei" title="Daye, Hubei">Daye, Hubei</a>, 2008)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When sex ratio began being studied in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> in 1960, it was still within the normal range. However, it climbed to 111.9 by 1990<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup> and to 118 by 2010 per its official census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Researchers believe that the causes of this sex ratio imbalance are increased female infant mortality, underreporting of female births and sex-selective abortion. According to Zeng et al. (1993), the most prominent cause is probably sex-selective abortion, but this is difficult to prove that in a country with little reliable birth data because of the hiding of “illegal” (under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One-Child_Policy" title="One-Child Policy">One-Child Policy</a>) births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zengyi93_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-zengyi93-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>These illegal births have led to underreporting of female infants. Zeng et al., using a reverse survival method, estimate that underreporting keeps about 2.26% male births and 5.94% female births off the books. Adjusting for unreported illegal births, they conclude that the corrected Chinese sex ratio at birth for 1989 was 111 rather than 115.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zengyi93_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-zengyi93-49">[49]</a></sup> These national averages over time, mask the regional sex ratio data. For example, in some provinces such as <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, sex ratio at birth is more than 130.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hesketh_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hesketh-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional Chinese techniques have been used to determine sex for hundreds of years, primarily with unknown accuracy. It was not until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultrasonography" title="Ultrasonography">ultrasonography</a> became widely available in urban and rural China that sex was able to be determined scientifically. In 1986, the Ministry of Health posted the Notice on Forbidding Prenatal Sex Determination, but it was not widely followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Three years later, the Ministry of Health outlawed the use of sex determination techniques, except for in diagnosing hereditary diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, many people have personal connections to medical practitioners and strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Son_preference" title="Son preference">son preference</a> still dominates culture, leading to the widespread use of sex determination techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup> According to Hardy, Gu, and Xie (2000), ultrasound has spread to all areas of China, as evidenced by the spread of the high sex ratio throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xie_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xie-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hardy, Gu, and Xie suggest sex-selective abortion is more prevalent in rural China because son preference is much stronger there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xie_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xie-54">[54]</a></sup> Urban areas of China, on average, are moving toward greater equality for both sexes, while rural China tends to follow more traditional views of gender. This is partially due to the belief that, while sons are always part of the family, daughters are only temporary, going to a new family when they marry. Additionally, if a woman’s firstborn child is a son, her position in society moves up, while the same is not true of a firstborn daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the past, desire for a son was manifested by large birth rates—many couples would continue to have children until they had a son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup> However, the combination of financial concerns and, more importantly, the <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">One-child policy</a> (discussed further below) have led to an increase in gender planning and selection. Even in rural areas, most women know that ultrasonography can be used for gender discernment. For each subsequent birth, Junhong found that women are over 10% more likely to have an ultrasound (39% for firstborn, 55% for second born, 67% for third born). Additionally, he found that the sex of the firstborn child impacts whether a woman will have an ultrasound in her subsequent pregnancies: 40% of women with a firstborn son have an ultrasound for their second born child, versus 70% of women with firstborn daughters. This points to a strong desire to select for a son if one has not been born yet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sex_ratio_at_birth_in_mainland_China.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="208" data-file-width="808" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Sex_ratio_at_birth_in_mainland_China.png/450px-Sex_ratio_at_birth_in_mainland_China.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Sex_ratio_at_birth_in_mainland_China.png/675px-Sex_ratio_at_birth_in_mainland_China.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/Sex_ratio_at_birth_in_mainland_China.png 2x" width="450"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sex_ratio_at_birth_in_mainland_China.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Birth sex ratios have dramatically changed in China since the implementation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One-Child_Policy" title="One-Child Policy">One-Child Policy</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of the lack of data about childbirth, a number of researchers have worked to learn about abortion statistics in China. One of the earliest studies by Qui (1987) found that according to cultural belief, fetuses are not thought of as human beings until they are born, leading to a cultural preference for abortion over infanticide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In fact, <a href="/wiki/Infanticide" title="Infanticide">infanticide</a> and infant abandonment are rather rare in China today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup> Instead, Junhong found that roughly 27% of women have an abortion. Additionally, he found that if a family’s firstborn was a girl, 92% of known female would-be second born fetuses were aborted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2005 study, Zhu, Lu, and Hesketh found that the highest sex ratio was for those ages 1–4, and two provinces, Tibet and Xinjiang, had sex ratios within normal limits. Two other provinces had a ratio over 140, four had ratios between 130-139, and seven had ratios between 120-129, each of which is significantly higher than the natural sex ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hesketh_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hesketh-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Variance in the one child policy has led to three types of provinces. Zhu et al. call Type 1, the most restrictive, policy where 40% of couples are permitted to have a second child but generally only if the first is a girl. In Type 2 provinces, any couple is permitted to have a second child if the first born is a girl or if the parents petition “hardship” and the petition is accepted by local officials. Type 3 provinces, typically sparsely populated, allow couples a second child and sometimes a third, irrespective of sex. Zhu et al. find that Type 2 provinces have the highest birth sex ratios, as seen in <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hesketh_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hesketh-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>High sex ratio trends in China is projected, by 2020, to create a pool of 55 million excess young adult men than women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> According to Junhong, many males between the ages of 28 and 49 are unable to find a partner and thus remain unmarried. Families in China are aware of the critical lack of female children and its implication on marriage prospects in the future; many parents are beginning to work extra when their sons are young so that they will be able to pay for a bride for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The birth sex ratio in China, according to a 2012 news report, has decreased to 117 males born for every 100 females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Female_foeticide_in_India" title="Female foeticide in India">Female foeticide in India</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1738" data-file-width="1578" height="385" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg/350px-2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg/525px-2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg/700px-2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2011_Census_sex_ratio_map_for_the_states_and_Union_Territories_of_India_Boys_to_Girls_0_to_1_age_group.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map of India's child sex ratio, 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>India’s 2001 census revealed a national 0–6 age child sex ratio of 108, which increased to 109 according to 2011 census (927 girls per 1000 boys and 919 girls per 1000 boys respectively, compared to expected normal ratio of 943 girls per 1000 boys).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The national average masks the variations in regional numbers according to 2011 census—Haryana’s ratio was 120, Punjab’s ratio was 118, Jammu &amp; Kashmir was 116, and Gujarat’s ratio was 111.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cc2011_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-cc2011-61">[61]</a></sup> The 2011 Census found eastern states of India had birth sex ratios between 103 and 104, lower than normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tec2011_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-tec2011-62">[62]</a></sup> In contrast to decadal nationwide census data, small non-random sample surveys report higher child sex ratios in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The child sex ratio in India shows a regional pattern. India’s 2011 census found that all eastern and southern states of India had a child sex ratio between 103 and 107,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cc2011_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-cc2011-61">[61]</a></sup> typically considered as the “natural ratio.” The highest sex ratios were observed in India's northern and northwestern states – <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a> (120), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> (118) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jammu_%26_Kashmir" title="Jammu &amp; Kashmir">Jammu &amp; Kashmir</a> (116).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ci20012011_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ci20012011-64">[64]</a></sup> The western states of Maharashtra and Rajasthan 2011 census found a child sex ratio of 113, Gujarat at 112 and Uttar Pradesh at 111.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ci20012011_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-ci20012011-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Indian census data suggests there is a positive correlation between abnormal sex ratio and better socio-economic status and literacy. Urban India has higher child sex ratio than rural India according to 1991, 2001 and 2011 Census data, implying higher prevalence of sex selective abortion in urban India. Similarly, child sex ratio greater than 115 boys per 100 girls is found in regions where the predominant majority is Hindu, Muslim, Sikh or Christian; furthermore "normal" child sex ratio of 104 to 106 boys per 100 girls are also found in regions where the predominant majority is Hindu, Muslim, Sikh or Christian. These data contradict any hypotheses that may suggest that sex selection is an archaic practice which takes place among uneducated, poor sections or particular religion of the Indian society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cc2011_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-cc2011-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbhindia_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-wbhindia-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6138" data-file-width="10263" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png/450px-India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png/675px-India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png/900px-India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png 2x" width="450"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India_Male_to_Female_Sex_Ratio_1941_1951_1961_1981_1991_2001_2011.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The male to female sex ratio for India, based on its official census data from 1941 through to 2011. The data suggests the existence of high sex ratios before and after the arrival of ultrasound-based prenatal care and sex screening technologies in India.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rutherford and Roy, in their 2003 paper, suggest that techniques for determining sex prenatally that were pioneered in the 1970s, gained popularity in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> These techniques, claim Rutherford and Roy, became broadly available in 17 of 29 Indian states by the early 2000s. Such prenatal sex determination techniques, claim Sudha and Rajan in a 1999 report, where available, favored male births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arnold, Kishor, and Roy, in their 2002 paper, too hypothesize that modern fetal sex screening techniques have skewed child sex ratios in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnold_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-68">[68]</a></sup> Ganatra et al., in their 2000 paper, use a small survey sample to estimate that <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>6</sub></span> of reported abortions followed a sex determination test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mevlude Akbulut-Yuksel and Daniel Rosenblum, in their 2012 paper, find that despite numerous publications and studies, there is limited formal evidence on the effects of the continued spread of ultrasound technology on missing women in India. They conclude, contrary to common belief, that the recent rapid spread of ultrasound in India, from the 1990s through 2000s, did not cause a concomitant rise in sex-selection and prenatal female abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maydrgermany_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-maydrgermany-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Indian government and various advocacy groups have continued the debate and discussion about ways to prevent sex selection. The immorality of prenatal sex selection has been questioned, with some arguments in favor of prenatal discrimination as more humane than postnatal discrimination by a family that does not want a female child. Others question whether the morality of sex selective abortion is any different over morality of abortion when there is no risk to the mother nor to the fetus, and abortion is used as a means to end an unwanted pregnancy?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>India passed its first abortion-related law, the so-called Medical Termination of Pregnancy Act of 1971, making abortion legal in most states, but specified legally acceptable reasons for abortion such as medical risk to mother and rape. The law also established physicians who can legally provide the procedure and the facilities where abortions can be performed, but did not anticipate sex selective abortion based on technology advances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>With increasing availability of sex screening technologies in India through the 1980s in urban India, and claims of its misuse, the Government of India passed the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Conception_and_Pre-Natal_Diagnostic_Techniques_Act,_1994" title="Pre-Conception and Pre-Natal Diagnostic Techniques Act, 1994">Pre-natal Diagnostic Techniques Act (PNDT)</a> in 1994. This law was further amended into the Pre-Conception and Pre-natal Diagnostic Techniques (Regulation and Prevention of Misuse) (PCPNDT) Act in 2004 to deter and punish prenatal sex screening and sex selective abortion. The impact of the law and its enforcement is unclear. <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a> and India's National Human Rights Commission, in 2009, asked the Government of India to assess the impact of the law. The Public Health Foundation of India, an activist NGO in its 2010 report, claimed a lack of awareness about the Act in parts of India, inactive role of the Appropriate Authorities, ambiguity among some clinics that offer prenatal care services, and the role of a few medical practitioners in disregarding the law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbhindia_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-wbhindia-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_and_Family_Welfare" title="Ministry of Health and Family Welfare">Ministry of Health and Family Welfare</a> of India has targeted education and media advertisements to reach clinics and medical professionals to increase awareness. The Indian Medical Association has undertaken efforts to prevent prenatal sex selection by giving its members <i>Beti Bachao</i> (save the daughter) badges during its meetings and conferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbhindia_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-wbhindia-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>MacPherson estimates that 100,000 abortions every year continue to be performed in India solely because the fetus is female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macpherson_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-macpherson-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Asian_countries">Other Asian countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other Asian countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other countries with large populations but high sex ratios include <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>. United Nations Population Fund, in its 2012 report,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> claims the birth sex ratio of Vietnam at 111 with its densely populated <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Delta" title="Red River Delta">Red River Delta</a> region at 116; for Pakistan, the UN estimates the birth sex ratio to be 110. The urban regions of Pakistan, particularly its densely populated region of Punjab, report a sex ratio above 112 (less than 900 females per 1000 males).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Hudson and Den Boer estimate the resulting deficit to be about 6 million missing girls in Pakistan than what would normally be expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hudsonboer_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-hudsonboer-77">[77]</a></sup> Three different research studies, according to Klausen and Wink, note that Pakistan had the world's highest % of missing girls, relative to its total pre-adult female population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> has reported a birth sex ratio of 108. <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> has reported a sex ratio at birth between 1.07 and 1.11 every year, across 4 million births, over the 20-year period from 1991 to 2011, with the highest birth sex ratios in the 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Sex-selective abortion is reported to be common in <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> too, but its incidence has declined in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> As of 2015, South Korea's sex ratio at birth was 1.07 male/female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-31">[31]</a></sup> In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> had a sex ratio at birth of 1.12 male/female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-31">[31]</a></sup> A 2001 study on births in the late 1990s concluded that "sex selection or sex-selective abortion might be practiced among Hong Kong Chinese women".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recently, a rise in the sex ratio at birth has been noted in some parts of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, most notably in the <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu_Valley" title="Kathmandu Valley">Kathmandu Valley</a>, but also in districts such as <a href="/wiki/Kaski_District" title="Kaski District">Kaski</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> High sex ratios at birth are most notable amongst richer, more educated sections of the population in urban areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Abnormal sex ratios at birth, possibly explained by growing incidence of sex-selective abortion, have also been noted in some other countries outside South and East Asia. According to the 2011 CIA estimates, countries with more than 110 males per 100 females at birth also include <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> and former Soviet republics of <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caucasus">Caucasus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Caucasus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="898" data-file-width="1155" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg/250px-Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg/375px-Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg/500px-Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caucasus_topographic_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Topography of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, a region at the border of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, situated between the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian</a> seas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> has been named a "male-dominated region", and as families have stated to become smaller in recent years, the pressures to have sons has increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Before the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a> in the early 1990s, the birth sex ratio in Caucasus countries such as <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> was in the 105 to 108 range. After the collapse, the birth sex ratios sharply climbed and have remained high for the last 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fmesle_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-fmesle-87">[87]</a></sup> Christian Armenia and Georgia, as well as Islamic Azerbaijan have seen strongly imbalanced birth sex ratios in the first decade of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teconomist_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-teconomist-39">[39]</a></sup> In Georgia, the birth sex ratio for the years 2005–2009 was about 120, a trend <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> claims suggests that the practice of sex-selective abortion in the Caucasus has been similar to those in East Asia and South Asia in recent decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teconomist_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-teconomist-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>For 2005–10 birth data, the sex ratio in Armenia is seen to be a function of birth order. Among couples having their first child, Armenia averages 138 boys for every 100 girls every year. If the first child is a son, the sex ratio of the second child of Armenian couple averages to be 85. If the first child is a daughter, the sex ratio of the second Armenian child averages to be 156 boys for 100 girls. Overall, the birth sex ratio for in Armenia exceeds 115, far higher than India's 108, claim scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teconomist_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-teconomist-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Bongaarts_2013_pages_185_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Bongaarts_2013_pages_185-89">[89]</a></sup> While these high birth sex ratios suggest sex-selective abortion, there is no direct evidence of observed large-scale sex-selective abortions in Caucasus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fmesle_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-fmesle-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Europe">Southeast Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Southeast Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An imbalanced birth sex ratio has been present in the 21st century in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Balkans" title="Western Balkans">Western Balkans</a>, in countries such as <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Macedonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>. As of 2016, according to CIA estimates, Albania has one the highest birth sex imbalances in the world, at 110.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-31">[31]</a></sup> According to Eurostat and birth record data over 2008–11, the birth sex ratios of Albania and Montenegro for that period were 112 and 110 respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpaaugust2012_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-unfpaaugust2012-38">[38]</a></sup> In recent years, the birth registration data for Macedonia and Kosovo indicate birth sex ratios above 108; for example, in 2011 the birth sex ratio was 108 in Macedonia, while in 2010 the birth sex ratio for Kosovo was 112.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Scholars claim this suggests that sex-selective abortions are becoming common in southeast Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-czgparis_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-czgparis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like in other countries, sex-selective abortion is difficult to track in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> because of lack of data.</p>
<p>While the majority of parents in United States do not practice sex-selective abortion, there is certainly a trend toward male preference. According to a 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallup_poll" title="Gallup poll">Gallup poll</a>, if they were only allowed to have one child, 40% of respondents said they would prefer a boy, while only 28% preferred a girl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> When told about prenatal sex selection techniques such as sperm sorting and in vitro fertilization embryo selection, 40% of Americans surveyed thought that picking embryos by sex was an acceptable manifestation of reproductive rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jesudason_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jesudason-94">[94]</a></sup> These selecting techniques are available at about half of American fertility clinics, as of 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, it is notable that minority groups that immigrate into the United States bring their cultural views and mindsets into the country with them. A study carried out at a Massachusetts infertility clinic shows that the majority of couples using these techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Preimplantation_genetic_diagnosis" title="Preimplantation genetic diagnosis">Preimplantation genetic diagnosis</a> came from a Chinese or Asian background. This is thought to branch from the social importance of giving birth to male children in China and other Asian countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study of the 2000 United States Census suggests possible male bias in families of Chinese, Korean and Indian immigrants, which was getting increasingly stronger in families where first one or two children were female. In those families where the first two children were girls, the birth sex ratio of the third child was 151.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of this movement toward sex preference and selection, many bans on sex-selective abortion have been proposed at the state and federal level. In 2010 and 2011, sex-selective abortions were banned in <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, respectively. Legislators in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> have also tried to pass acts banning the procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jesudason_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jesudason-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_countries">Other countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A 2013 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Bongaarts_2013_pages_185_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-John_Bongaarts_2013_pages_185-89">[89]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/John_Bongaarts" title="John Bongaarts">John Bongaarts</a> based on surveys in 61 major countries calculates the sex ratios that would result if parents had the number of sons and daughters they want. In 35 countries, claims Bongaarts, the desired birth sex ratio in respective countries would be more than 110 boys for every 100 girls if parents in these countries actually get a gender what they hope for (higher than India’s, which The Economist claims is 108).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teconomist_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-teconomist-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Estimates_of_missing_women">Estimates of missing women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Estimates of missing women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Estimates of implied missing girls, considering the "normal" birth sex ratio to be the 103–107 range, vary considerably between researchers and underlying assumptions for expected post-birth mortality rates for men and women. For example, a 2005 study estimated that over 90 million females were "missing" from the expected population in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> alone, and suggested that sex-selective abortion plays a role in this deficit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gettis_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gettis-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hudsonboer_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-hudsonboer-77">[77]</a></sup> For early 1990s, Sen estimated 107 million missing women, Coale estimated 60 million as missing, while Klasen estimated 89 million missing women in China, India, Pakistan, Bangladesh, Nepal, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KlausenWink_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-KlausenWink-12">[12]</a></sup> Guilmoto,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-czg_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-czg-98">[98]</a></sup> in his 2010 report, uses recent data (except for Pakistan), and estimates a much lower number of missing girls, but notes that the higher sex ratios in numerous countries have created a gender gap – shortage of girls – in the 0–19 age group.</p>
