<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reasons_for_sex-selective_abortion">Reasons for sex-selective abortion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reasons for sex-selective abortion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Various theories have been proposed as possible reasons for sex-selective abortion. Culture rather than economic conditions is favored by some researchers because such deviations in sex ratios do not exist in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gettis_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gettis-2">[2]</a></sup> Other hypotheses include disparate gender-biased access to resources,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macpherson_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-macpherson-74">[74]</a></sup> and attempts to control population growth such as using one child policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hesketh_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hesketh-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some demographers question whether sex-selective abortion or infanticide claims are accurate, because underreporting of female births may also explain high sex ratios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Natural reasons may also explain some of the abnormal sex ratios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James-JournalofEndocrinology_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-James-JournalofEndocrinology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rjhmam_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-rjhmam-16">[16]</a></sup> In contrast to these possible causes of abnormal sex ratio, Klasen and Wink suggest India and China’s high sex ratios are primarily the result of sex-selective abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KlausenWink_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-KlausenWink-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_preference">Cultural preference</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cultural preference">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The reason for intensifying sex-selection abortion in China and India can be seen through history and cultural background. Generally, before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_era" title="Information era">information era</a>, male babies were preferred because they provided manual labor and continuation of the family <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a>. Labor is still important in developing nations as China and India, but when it comes to family lineage, it is of great importance.</p>
<p>The selective abortion of female fetuses is most common in areas where cultural norms value male children over female children for a variety of social and economic reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodkind_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goodkind-1">[1]</a></sup> A son is often preferred as an "asset" since he can earn and support the family; a daughter is a "liability" since she will be married off to another family, and so will not contribute financially to her parents. Sex selective female abortion is a continuation, in a different form, of a practice of <a href="/wiki/Female_infanticide" title="Female infanticide">female infanticide</a> or withholding of postnatal health care for girls in certain households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_2005_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta_2005-101">[101]</a></sup> Furthermore, in some cultures sons are expected to take care of their parents in their old age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> These factors are complicated by the effect of diseases on child sex ratio, where communicable and noncommunicable diseases affect males and females differently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_2005_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta_2005-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>In modern East Asia, a large part of the pattern of preferences leading to this practice can be condensed simply as a desire to have a male heir. Monica Das Gupta (2005) observes, from 1989 birth data for China, there was no evidence of selective abortion of female fetuses among firstborn children. However, there was a strong preference for a boy if the first born was a girl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_2005_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta_2005-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disparate_gendered_access_to_resources">Disparate gendered access to resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Disparate gendered access to resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although there is significant evidence of the prevalence of sex-selective abortions in many nations (especially India and China), there is also evidence to suggest that some of the variation in global sex ratios is due to disparate access to resources. As MacPherson (2007) notes, there can be significant differences in gender violence and access to food, healthcare, immunizations between male and female children. This leads to high infant and childhood mortality among girls, which causes changes in sex ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macpherson_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-macpherson-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Disparate, gendered access to resources appears to be strongly linked to socioeconomic status. Specifically, poorer families are sometimes forced to ration food, with daughters typically receiving less priority than sons (Klasen and Wink 2003).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KlausenWink_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-KlausenWink-12">[12]</a></sup> However, Klasen’s 2001 study revealed that this practice is less common in the poorest families, but rises dramatically in the slightly less poor families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KlausenWink_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-KlausenWink-12">[12]</a></sup> Klasen and Wink’s 2003 study suggests that this is “related to greater female economic independence and fewer cultural strictures among the poorest sections of the population.” In other words, the poorest families are typically less bound by cultural expectations and norms, and women tend to have more freedom to become family breadwinners out of necessity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KlausenWink_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-KlausenWink-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Increased sex ratios can be caused by disparities in aspects of life other than vital resources. According to Sen (1990), differences in wages and job advancement also have a dramatic effect on sex ratios. This is why high sex ratios are sometimes seen in nations with little sex-selective abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen1990_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sen1990-13">[13]</a></sup> Additionally, high female education rates are correlated with lower sex ratios (World Bank 2011).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lopez and Ruzikah (1983) found that, when given the same resources, women tend to outlive men at all stages of life after infancy. However, globally, resources are not always allocated equitably. Thus, some scholars argue that disparities in access to resources such as healthcare, education, and nutrition play at least a small role in the high sex ratios seen in some parts of the world (Klasen and Wink 2003). For example, Alderman and Gerter (1997) found that unequal access to healthcare is a primary cause of female death in developing nations, especially in Southeast Asia. Moreover, in India, lack of equal access to healthcare has led to increased disease and higher rates of female mortality in every age group until the late thirties (Sen 1990). This is particularly noteworthy because, in regions of the world where women receive equal resources, women tend to outlive men (Sen 1990).</p>
<p>Economic disadvantage alone may not always lead to increased sex ratio, claimed Sen in 1990. For example, in sub-Saharan Africa, one of the most economically disadvantaged regions of the world, there is an excess of women. So, if economic disadvantage is uncorrelated with sex ratio in Africa, some other factor(s) may be at play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen1990_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sen1990-13">[13]</a></sup> More detailed analysis of African demographics, in 2002, suggests that Africa too has wide variation in birth sex ratios (from 1.01 in Bantu populations of East Africa to 1.08 in Nigeria and Ethiopia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Thus economic disadvantage remains a possible unresolved hypothesis for Africa as well.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="One-child_policy">One-child policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: One-child policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the 1949 creation of the People's Republic of China, the issue of population control came into the national spotlight. In the early years of the Republic, leaders believed that telling citizens to reduce their fertility was enough, repealing laws banning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraception" title="Contraception">contraception</a> and instead promoting its use. However, the contraceptives were not widely available, both because of lack of supply and because of cultural taboo against discussing sex. Efforts were slowed following the famine of 1959–61 but were resumed shortly thereafter with virtually the same results. Then, in 1964, the Family Planning Office was established to enforce stricter guidelines regarding fertility and it was moderately successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1979, the government adopted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One-Child_Policy" title="One-Child Policy">One-Child Policy</a>, which limited many families to one child, unless specified by provincial regulations. It was instituted as an attempt to boost the Chinese economy. Under it, families who break rules regarding the number of children they are allowed are given various punishments (primarily monetary), dependent upon the province in which they live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>As stated above, the sex ratios of a province are largely determined by the type of restriction placed upon the family, pointing to the conclusion that much of the imbalance in sex ratio in China can be attributed to the policy. Research by Junhong (2001) found that many parents are willing to pay to ensure that their child is male (especially if their first child is female), but will not do the same to ensure their child is female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-9"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup> Likely, fear of the harsh monetary punishments of the One-Child Policy make ensuring a son’s birth a smart investment. Therefore, son’s cultural and economic importance to families and the large expenses associated with multiple children are primary factors leading to China’s disparate sex ratio.</p>
<p>In 2013, China announced plans to formally change the One-Child policy, making it less stringent. The National People’s Congress has changed the policy to allow couples to have two children, so long as one of the partners is an only child. This change was not sparked by sex ratios, but rather by an aging population that is causing the workforce to grow increasingly smaller. It is estimated that this new law will lead to two million more births per year and could cause a baby boom in China. Unfortunately, many of China’s social problems are based on overpopulation. So, it is unclear if this new law will actually lead to women being more valued in Chinese society as the number of citizens increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trivers.E2.80.93Willard_hypothesis">Trivers–Willard hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Trivers–Willard hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trivers%E2%80%93Willard_hypothesis" title="Trivers–Willard hypothesis">Trivers–Willard hypothesis</a> argues that available resources affect male <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_success" title="Reproductive success">reproductive success</a> more than female and that consequently parents should prefer males when resources are plentiful and females when resources are scarce. This has been applied to resource differences between individuals in a society and also to resource differences between societies. Empirical evidence is mixed with higher support in better studies according to Cronk in a 2007 review. One example, in a 1997 study, of a group with a preference for females was <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Romani</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, a low status group. They "had a female-biased sex ratio at birth, were more likely to abort a fetus after having had one or more daughters, nursed their daughters longer, and sent their daughters to school for longer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal_effects">Societal effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Societal effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Missing_women">Missing women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Missing women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The idea of “missing women” was first suggested by Amartya Sen, one of the first scholars to study high sex ratios and their causes globally, in 1990. In order to illustrate the gravity of the situation, he calculated the number of women that were not alive because of sex-selective abortion or discriminatory practices. He found that there were 11 percent fewer women than there “should” have been, if China had the natural sex ratio. This figure, when combined with statistics from around the world, led to a finding of over 100 million missing women. In other words, by the early 1990s, the number of missing women was “larger than the combined casualties of all famines in the twentieth century” (Sen 1990).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen1990_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sen1990-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>This has led to particular concern due to a critical shortage of wives. In some rural areas, there is already a shortage of women, which is tied to migration into urban areas (Park and Cho 1995).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkCho_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParkCho-109">[109]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, high male sex ratios and declining birth rates over several decades have led to cross-cultural marriage between local men and foreign women from countries such as mainland China, Vietnam and the Philippines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> However, sex-selective abortion is not the only cause of this phenomenon; it is also related to migration and declining fertility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkCho_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-ParkCho-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trafficking_and_sex_work">Trafficking and sex work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Trafficking and sex work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some scholars argue that as the proportion of women to men decreases globally, there will be an increase in <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">trafficking</a> and sex work (both forced and self-elected), as many people will be willing to do more to obtain a sexual partner (Junhong 2001).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-10"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup> Already, there are reports of women from <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> systematically trafficked to mainland <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and sold into forced marriages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hvistendahl_WSJ_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hvistendahl_WSJ-111">[111]</a></sup> Moreover, Ullman and Fidell (1989) suggested that pornography and sex-related crimes of violence (i.e., rape and molestation) would also increase with an increasing sex ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Widening_of_the_gender_social_gap">Widening of the gender social gap</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Widening of the gender social gap">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As Park and Cho (1995) note, families in areas with high sex ratios that have mostly sons tend to be smaller than those with mostly daughters (because the families with mostly sons appear to have used sex-selective techniques to achieve their “ideal” composition).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkCho_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-ParkCho-109">[109]</a></sup> Particularly in poor areas, large families tend to have more problems with resource allocation, with daughters often receiving fewer resources than sons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkCho_109-3"><a href="#cite_note-ParkCho-109">[109]</a></sup> Blake (1989) is credited for noting the relationship between family size and childhood “quality.” Therefore, if families with daughters continue to be predominantly large, it is likely that the social gap between genders will widen due to traditional cultural discrimination and lack of resource availability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Guttentag and Secord (1983) hypothesized that when the proportion of males throughout the world is greater, there is likely to be more violence and war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guttentag_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guttentag-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_positive_effects">Potential positive effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Potential positive effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some scholars believe that when sex ratios are high, women actually become valued more because of their relative shortage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkCho_109-4"><a href="#cite_note-ParkCho-109">[109]</a></sup> Park and Cho (1995) suggest that as women become more scarce, they may have “increased value for conjugal and reproductive functions” (75). Eventually, this could lead to better social conditions, followed by the birth of more women and sex ratios moving back to natural levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkCho_109-5"><a href="#cite_note-ParkCho-109">[109]</a></sup> This claim is supported by the work of demographer Nathan Keifitz. Keifitz (1983) wrote that as women become fewer, their relative position in society will increase. However, to date, no data has supported this claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been suggested by Belanger (2002) that sex-selective abortion may have positive effects on the mother choosing to abort the female fetus. This is related to the historical duty of mothers to produce a son in order to carry on the family name. As previously mentioned, women gain status in society when they have a male child, but not when they have a female child. Oftentimes, bearing of a son leads to greater legitimacy and agency for the mother. In some regions of the world where son preference is especially strong, sonless women are treated as outcasts. In this way, sex-selective abortion is a way for women to select for male fetuses, helping secure greater family status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goodkind (1999)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> argues that sex-selective abortion should not be banned purely because of its discriminatory nature. Instead, he argues, we must consider the overall lifetime possibilities of discrimination. In fact, it is possible that sex-selective abortion takes away much of the discrimination women would face later in life. Since families have the option of selecting for the fetal sex they desire, if they choose not to abort a female fetus, she is more likely to be valued later in life. In this way, sex-selective abortion may be a more humane alternative to infanticide, abandonment, or neglect. Goodkind (1999) poses an essential philosophical question, “if a ban were enacted against prenatal sex testing (or the use of abortion for sex-selective purposes), how many excess postnatal deaths would a society be willing to tolerate in lieu of whatever sex-selective abortions were avoided?”</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex-selective_abortion_in_the_context_of_abortion">Sex-selective abortion in the context of abortion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Sex-selective abortion in the context of abortion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>MacPherson estimates that 100,000 sex-selective abortions every year continue to be performed in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macpherson_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-macpherson-74">[74]</a></sup> For a contrasting perspective, in the United States with a population <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>th of India, over 1.2 million abortions every year were performed between 1990 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> with a population <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>20</sub></span>th of India, over 189,000 abortions were performed in 2011, or a yearly rate of 17.5 abortions per 1,000 women aged 15–44.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The average for the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> was 30 abortions per year per 1,000 women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many scholars have noted the difficulty in reconciling the discriminatory nature of sex-selective abortion with the right of women to have control over their own bodies. This conflict manifests itself primarily when discussing laws about sex-selective abortion. Weiss (1995:205) writes: "The most obvious challenge sex-selective abortion represents for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pro-choice" title="Pro-choice">pro-choice</a> feminists is the difficulty of reconciling a pro-choice position with moral objections one might have to sex selective abortion (especially since it has been used primarily on female fetuses), much less the advocacy of a law banning sex-selective abortion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> As a result, arguments both for and against sex-selective abortion are typically highly reflective of one’s own personal beliefs about abortion in general. Warren (1985:104) argues that there is a difference between acting within one’s rights and acting upon the most morally sound choice, implying that sex-selective abortion might be within rights but not morally sound. Warren also notes that, if we are to ever reverse the trend of sex-selective abortion and high sex ratios, we must work to change the patriarchy-based society which breeds the strong son preference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_and_initiatives_against_sex-selective_abortion">Laws and initiatives against sex-selective abortion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Laws and initiatives against sex-selective abortion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sex_determination_ban.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Sex_determination_ban.JPG/320px-Sex_determination_ban.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Sex_determination_ban.JPG/480px-Sex_determination_ban.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Sex_determination_ban.JPG/640px-Sex_determination_ban.JPG 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sex_determination_ban.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sign in an Indian hospital stating that prenatal sex determination is a crime.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws">Laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1994 over 180 states signed the Programme of Action of the <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Population_and_Development" title="International Conference on Population and Development">International Conference on Population and Development</a>, agreeing to "eliminate all forms of discrimination against the girl child".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> In 2011 the resolution of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Assembly_of_the_Council_of_Europe" title="Parliamentary Assembly of the Council of Europe">PACE</a>'s Committee on Equal Opportunities for Women and Men condemned the practice of prenatal sex selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_policy_initiatives">Media and policy initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex-selective_abortion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Media and policy initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many nations have attempted to address sex-selective abortion rates through a combination of media campaigns and policy initiatives.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Canada</dt>
</dl>
<p>In Canada, a group of MPs led by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Warawa" title="Mark Warawa">Mark Warawa</a> are working on having the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Parliament" title="Canadian Parliament">Parliament</a> pass a resolution condemning sex-selective pregnancy termination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>USA</dt>
</dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> has debated legislation that would outlaw the practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The legislation ultimately failed to pass in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the state level, laws against sex-selective abortions have been passed in a number of US states;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christie20130529_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-christie20130529-129">[129]</a></sup> the law passed in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> in 2011 prohibits both sex-selective and race-selective abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christie20130529_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-christie20130529-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>United Kingdom</dt>
</dl>
<p>The law on sex-selective abortion is unresolved in the United Kingdom. In order for an abortion to be legal, doctors need to show that continuing the pregnancy could threaten the physical or mental health of the mother. In a recent case, two doctors were caught on camera offering a sex-selective abortion but the Director of Public Prosecution deemed it not in the public interest to proceed with the prosecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Following this incidence, MPs voted 181 to 1 for a Bill put forward by <a href="/wiki/Tessa_Munt" title="Tessa Munt">Tessa Munt</a> and 11 other MPs aiming to end confusion about the legality of this practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Organisations such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BPAS" title="BPAS">BPAS</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abortion_Rights_(Organisation)" title="Abortion Rights (Organisation)">Abortion Rights</a> have been lobbying for the decriminalisation of sex-selective abortions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>China</dt>
</dl>
<p>China’s government has increasingly recognized its role in a reduction of the national sex ratio. As a result, since 2005, it has sponsored a “boys and girls are equal campaign.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JianSong_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-JianSong-137">[137]</a></sup> For example, in 2000, the Chinese government began the “Care for Girls” Initiative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Furthermore, several levels of government have been modified to protect the “political, economic, cultural, and social” rights of women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JianSong_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-JianSong-137">[137]</a></sup> Finally, the Chinese government has enacted policies and interventions to help reduce the sex ratio at birth. In 2005, sex-selective abortion was made illegal in China. This came in response to the ever-increasing sex ratio and a desire to try to detract from it and reach a more normal ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The sex ratio among firstborn children in urban areas from 2000 to 2005 didn’t rise at all, so there is hope that this movement is taking hold across the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junhong_9-11"><a href="#cite_note-Junhong-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>UNICEF and UNFPA have partnered with the Chinese government and grassroots-level women’s groups such as All China Women’s Federation to promote gender equality in policy and practice, as well engage various social campaigns to help lower birth sex ratio and to reduce excess female child mortality rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>India</dt>
</dl>
<p>In India, according to a 2007 study by MacPherson, Prenatal Diagnostic Techniques Act (PCPNDT Act) was highly publicized by NGOs and the government. Many of the ads used depicted abortion as violent, creating fear of abortion itself within the population. The ads focused on the religious and moral shame associated with abortion. MacPherson claims this media campaign was not effective because some perceived this as an attack on their character, leading to many becoming closed off, rather than opening a dialogue about the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macpherson_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-macpherson-74">[74]</a></sup> This emphasis on morality, claims MacPherson, increased fear and shame associated with all abortions, leading to an increase in unsafe abortions in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macpherson_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-macpherson-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The government of India, in a 2011 report, has begun better educating all stakeholders about its MTP and PCPNDT laws. In its communication campaigns, it is clearing up public misconceptions by emphasizing that sex determination is illegal, but abortion is legal for certain medical conditions in India. The government is also supporting implementation of programs and initiatives that seek to reduce gender discrimination, including media campaign to address the underlying social causes of sex selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbhindia_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-wbhindia-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other recent policy initiatives adopted by numerous states of India, claims Guilmoto,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-czg_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-czg-98">[98]</a></sup> attempt to address the assumed economic disadvantage of girls by offering support to girls and their parents. These policies provide <a href="/wiki/Conditional_cash_transfer" title="Conditional cash transfer">conditional cash transfer</a> and scholarships only available to girls, where payments to a girl and her parents are linked to each stage of her life, such as when she is born, completion of her childhood immunization, her joining school at grade 1, her completing school grades 6, 9 and 12, her marriage past age 21. Some states are offering higher pension benefits to parents who raise one or two girls. Different states of India have been experimenting with various innovations in their girl-driven welfare policies. For example, the state of Delhi adopted a pro-girl policy initiative (locally called <i>Laadli scheme</i>), which initial data suggests may be lowering the birth sex ratio in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-czg_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-czg-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-unfpa2012-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-unfpa2012_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-unfpa2012_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.unfpa.org/webdav/site/global/shared/documents/publications/2012/Report_SexRatios_2012.pdf" rel="nofollow">Report of the International Workshop on Skewed Sex Ratios at Birth</a> United Nations FPA (2012)</span></li>
